  • Between exercises: Active rest between sets
  • End of workout: When fresh muscles not needed
  • Separate session: Dedicated core work

Benefits of Core Stability:

Performance:

  • Better force transfer in sports and activities
  • Improved balance and coordination
  • Enhanced power production
  • More efficient movement patterns

Injury Prevention:

  • Low back pain: Strong core reduces risk
  • Proper mechanics: Maintains neutral spine under load
  • Joint protection: Reduces compensatory movements

Functional Benefits:

  • Improved posture
  • Easier daily activities (lifting, carrying, bending)
  • Better balance (especially important for older adults)
  • Reduced fall risk

Special Population Considerations:

Low Back Pain:

  • Core stability exercises often HELP low back pain
  • Start with easier variations
  • Focus on maintaining neutral spine
  • May need physical therapy clearance if acute pain

Pregnancy:

  • 1st trimester: Can usually perform standard plank
  • 2nd-3rd trimester: Modify to incline or standing plank
  • Avoid: Prone exercises in later pregnancy
  • Focus on: Pelvic floor engagement

Older Adults:

  • Start with wall or incline planks
  • Progress slowly
  • Balance training equally important
  • May need modifications for wrist or shoulder issues

Assessment of Core Stability:

Plank Hold Test:

  • Procedure: Hold proper plank position as long as possible
  • Standards (approximate):
    • Excellent: >2 minutes
    • Good: 1-2 minutes
    • Average: 30-60 seconds
    • Below average: <30 seconds
  • End test when: Form breaks down (hips sag, pike position, pain)

Exam Tips:

üéØ Critical facts for exam:

  • Plank is THE classic core stability exercise ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Isometric exercise: No movement, static hold
  • Works ALL core muscles: Rectus abdominis, obliques, transverse abdominis, erector spinae
  • Anti-extension pattern: Resists lumbar spine extension
  • Safe and effective: Appropriate for most populations
  • Other good options: Side plank, bird dog, dead bug, Pallof press
  • NOT good options: Leg extension, bicep curl, seated machine exercises
  • Progression: Wall ‚Üí Incline ‚Üí Knee ‚Üí Standard ‚Üí Advanced variations

Sample Exam Questions:

Q: Which exercise best develops core stability?

A: Plank ‚úì

Q: What type of muscle contraction occurs during a plank hold?

A: Isometric (static, no movement) ‚úì

Q: What is the primary function trained during a plank?

A: Maintaining neutral spine position against gravity (anti-extension) ‚úì

Question 107: EPOC - Excess Post-Exercise Oxygen Consumption

What does EPOC stand for?
‚úì CORRECT ANSWER:
Excess Post-exercise Oxygen Consumption

Extended Explanation:

EPOC is a fundamental concept in exercise physiology that explains why you continue burning calories after exercise ends. Understanding EPOC is essential for both exam success and practical application in weight management and training programs.

EPOC Defined:

Full Term:

  • Excess Post-exercise Oxygen Consumption ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Also called: "Oxygen debt" (older term) or "Afterburn effect" (popular term)

What It Means:

  • Elevated oxygen consumption AFTER exercise ends
  • Body continues using more oxygen than resting level
  • Can last minutes to hours depending on exercise intensity and duration
  • Results in additional calorie expenditure post-exercise

Why EPOC Occurs - Physiological Mechanisms:

1. ATP-PCr Replenishment:

  • What depleted: Phosphocreatine stores used during exercise
  • Must be restored: Requires oxygen and energy
  • Timeline: Mostly complete within 3-5 minutes
  • Fast component of EPOC

2. Lactate Removal:

  • Accumulated during intense exercise: Must be cleared
  • Pathways:
    • Converted to glucose (gluconeogenesis in liver)
    • Oxidized for energy
    • Converted to glycogen
  • Requires oxygen: Contributes to elevated O‚ÇÇ consumption
  • Timeline: 30 minutes to 1 hour typically

3. Elevated Body Temperature:

  • Core temp rises during exercise: 1-3¬∞C increase typical
  • Cooling requires energy: Sweating, increased blood flow to skin
  • Metabolic rate elevated: Q10 effect (10% increase per 1¬∞C)
  • Timeline: Can persist 30-60 minutes

4. Hormone Effects:

  • Elevated catecholamines: Epinephrine and norepinephrine remain high
  • Increased metabolic rate: These hormones increase metabolism
  • Timeline: Can remain elevated several hours

5. Elevated Heart Rate & Breathing:

  • Cardiovascular system: Remains elevated to support recovery
  • Ventilation: Breathing rate and depth above resting
  • Requires energy: Heart and respiratory muscles working

6. Tissue Repair & Protein Synthesis:

  • Muscle damage from exercise: Especially resistance training
  • Repair processes: Energy-intensive
  • Timeline: Can continue 24-48+ hours
  • Slow component of EPOC

EPOC Magnitude - How Much Extra Calorie Burn?

Fast Component (First 1-2 Hours):

  • Moderate exercise (60 min): 50-100 extra kcal
  • Vigorous exercise (30 min): 50-150 extra kcal
  • HIIT session (20 min): 100-200 extra kcal

Slow Component (Next 12-48 Hours):

  • Resistance training: Additional 50-200 kcal over 24-48 hours
  • Very intense/long training: Can be higher
  • Highly variable: Individual differences significant

Total EPOC Effect:

  • Typically adds: 6-15% to total calories burned during exercise
  • Example: Burn 300 kcal during workout + 20-45 kcal from EPOC
  • Meaningful but modest: Not as dramatic as some marketing claims suggest

Factors That Increase EPOC:

Exercise Intensity (MOST IMPORTANT):

  • Vigorous exercise: Much greater EPOC than moderate ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • HIIT: Produces significant EPOC despite short duration
  • Near-maximal effort: Greatest EPOC response
  • Mechanism: Greater metabolic disturbance = more recovery needed

Exercise Duration:

  • Longer workouts: Greater EPOC
  • Threshold: >30 minutes typically needed for significant EPOC
  • Very long (>90 min): Can have prolonged elevated metabolism

Exercise Type:

  • Resistance training: High EPOC (muscle damage, protein synthesis)
  • HIIT/sprints: Very high EPOC
  • Steady-state moderate cardio: Low-moderate EPOC
  • Circuit training: Moderate-high EPOC

Training Status:

  • Untrained individuals: Higher EPOC initially
  • Trained individuals: More efficient, lower EPOC for same absolute workload
  • BUT: Trained people can work at higher intensities (greater EPOC)

EPOC and Weight Management:

Practical Application:

  • Additional calories burned: Can contribute to weight loss
  • Long-term effect: Cumulative benefit over weeks/months
  • Not a magic bullet: Exercise during session still burns most calories

Realistic Expectations:

  • Don't overestimate: Marketing often exaggerates "afterburn"
  • Real benefit: Maybe 50-200 extra kcal depending on workout
  • Focus on total: During exercise + EPOC = total calorie expenditure

Best for EPOC:

  • High-intensity interval training (HIIT)
  • Heavy resistance training
  • Circuit training with minimal rest
  • Sprint intervals

Measuring EPOC:

Research Methods:

  • Indirect calorimetry: Measure oxygen consumption post-exercise
  • Compare to baseline: Resting VO‚ÇÇ vs. post-exercise VO‚ÇÇ
  • Calculate area under curve: Total excess oxygen consumed

Practical (Non-Lab):

  • Heart rate monitors: Track elevated HR post-exercise
  • Not direct measurement: But correlates with elevated metabolism
  • Wearables: Some estimate EPOC (varying accuracy)

EPOC Time Course:

Typical Timeline:

Time Post-Exercise Metabolic Status Primary Mechanisms
0-3 minutes Very elevated PCr replenishment, elevated HR/breathing
3-30 minutes Elevated Lactate clearance, temperature, hormones
30-120 minutes Moderately elevated Continued temperature regulation, hormones
2-48 hours Slightly elevated Tissue repair, protein synthesis (mainly after resistance training)

EPOC Compared to During-Exercise Calories:

Perspective:

  • During 30-min vigorous run: ~350 kcal
  • EPOC after: ~50-75 kcal
  • Total: ~400-425 kcal
  • EPOC adds ~15%: Helpful but not huge

Don't Ignore It:

  • 50-100 extra kcal/day √ó 365 days = 18,000-36,500 kcal/year
  • Equals 5-10 lbs of fat theoretically
  • Cumulative effect meaningful over time

EPOC and Training Adaptations:

How Training Affects EPOC:

  • Efficiency improves: Same absolute workout = less EPOC
  • Can work harder: Trained individuals achieve higher intensities
  • Net effect: Can still achieve high EPOC by working harder
  • Adaptation: Body becomes better at recovery

Common Misconceptions:

  • ‚ùå "Afterburn burns hundreds of calories for days": Exaggerated; typically 50-200 kcal total
  • ‚ùå "All exercise creates same EPOC": Intensity matters most
  • ‚ùå "EPOC replaces need for long cardio": Both have value; not either/or
  • ‚ùå "Light exercise has high EPOC": Low-intensity = minimal EPOC
  • ‚úì Reality: EPOC is real but modest; helpful component of total calorie expenditure

Exam Tips:

üéØ Critical facts for exam:

  • EPOC = Excess Post-exercise Oxygen Consumption ‚úì‚úì‚úì (MUST know full term)
  • Also called "oxygen debt" or "afterburn effect"
  • Elevated oxygen consumption AFTER exercise ends
  • Results in additional calorie burn post-exercise
  • Intensity most important factor: Higher intensity = greater EPOC
  • HIIT and resistance training: Produce highest EPOC
  • Mechanisms: ATP-PCr replenishment, lactate removal, elevated temperature, hormone effects, tissue repair
  • Duration: Can last minutes to 48+ hours depending on workout
  • Magnitude: Typically adds 6-15% to calories burned during exercise
  • Practical benefit: Real but modest; don't overestimate

Sample Exam Questions:

Q: What does EPOC stand for?

A: Excess Post-exercise Oxygen Consumption ‚úì

Q: What type of exercise produces the greatest EPOC?

A: High-intensity interval training (HIIT) or vigorous resistance training ‚úì

Q: What is the primary factor determining EPOC magnitude?

A: Exercise intensity ‚úì

Q: How long can EPOC last after intense exercise?

A: Minutes to hours (fast component) or up to 24-48 hours (slow component from resistance training) ‚úì

Question 108: Protein Intake for Endurance Athletes

Recommended protein intake for endurance athletes?
‚úì CORRECT ANSWER:
1.2-1.4 g/kg body weight

Extended Explanation:

Protein needs vary significantly based on activity type, training volume, and goals. Understanding appropriate protein recommendations for different populations is essential for nutrition counseling and exam success.

Protein Recommendations by Activity Level:

Sedentary Adults (RDA):

  • Intake: 0.8 g/kg body weight per day
  • Example: 70 kg person = 56 g protein/day
  • Purpose: Maintain nitrogen balance, basic tissue maintenance
  • Sufficient for: Non-active individuals

Endurance Athletes:

  • Intake: 1.2-1.4 g/kg body weight per day ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Example: 70 kg runner = 84-98 g protein/day
  • Why higher than RDA:
    • Muscle damage from prolonged activity
    • Some protein oxidized for energy during long exercise
    • Adaptation to training stimulus
    • Recovery between training sessions
  • Activities: Running, cycling, swimming, triathlon, cross-country skiing

Strength/Power Athletes:

  • Intake: 1.6-2.0 g/kg body weight per day
  • Example: 80 kg bodybuilder = 128-160 g protein/day
  • Why highest:
    • Muscle hypertrophy requires protein synthesis
    • Muscle damage from heavy resistance training
    • Maintaining lean mass during cutting phases
  • Activities: Bodybuilding, powerlifting, Olympic weightlifting

Recreational Exercisers:

  • Intake: 1.0-1.2 g/kg body weight per day
  • Appropriate for: General fitness, moderate training
  • Between RDA and athlete needs

Why Endurance Athletes Need More Protein:

Muscle Damage & Repair:

  • Long-duration exercise causes microtrauma
  • Repetitive muscle contractions create damage
  • Protein needed for repair processes
  • Especially important after hard training

Protein Oxidation During Exercise:

  • During prolonged exercise: 5-10% of energy can come from protein
  • Particularly when:
    • Glycogen depleted
    • Low carbohydrate availability
    • Very long duration (>90-120 min)
  • Branched-chain amino acids (BCAAs): Oxidized preferentially
  • Must be replaced: Through dietary intake

Adaptation to Training:

  • Mitochondrial biogenesis requires protein
  • Enzyme production increased
  • Capillary development
  • Red blood cell production

Recovery Between Sessions:

  • Endurance athletes often train daily or twice daily
  • Limited recovery time between sessions
  • Adequate protein speeds recovery
  • Maintains performance across training blocks

Protein Timing for Endurance Athletes:

Throughout the Day:

  • Distribution: 20-40g protein per meal, 3-4 meals
  • Better than: One large bolus
  • Reason: Maximizes muscle protein synthesis
  • Example day (70 kg athlete, 98g total):
    • Breakfast: 25g
    • Lunch: 30g
    • Snack: 15g
    • Dinner: 28g

Post-Exercise Window:

  • Timing: Within 30-60 minutes after training
  • Amount: 20-25g high-quality protein
  • Combined with carbs: 3:1 or 4:1 carb:protein ratio optimal
  • Purpose: Kickstart recovery, replenish glycogen
  • Example: Chocolate milk, protein shake with banana

Before Bed:

  • Casein protein: Slow-digesting, sustains overnight
  • Amount: 20-40g
  • Benefit: Reduces overnight muscle breakdown
  • Example: Greek yogurt, cottage cheese, casein shake

Protein Quality Matters:

High-Quality (Complete) Proteins:

  • Contain all essential amino acids
  • Animal sources: Meat, poultry, fish, eggs, dairy
  • Some plant sources: Soy, quinoa
  • Highest bioavailability

Lower-Quality (Incomplete) Proteins:

  • Missing one or more essential amino acids
  • Most plant proteins: Beans, lentils, nuts, grains
  • Solution: Combine complementary proteins (rice + beans)
  • Can meet needs: With variety and adequate total intake

Protein and Weight Management:

During Weight Loss:

  • Higher protein helpful: 1.6-2.4 g/kg for some endurance athletes
  • Preserves lean mass: During caloric deficit
  • Increases satiety: Feel fuller longer
  • Higher thermic effect: Burns more calories digesting protein

Special Considerations:

Ultra-Endurance Athletes:

  • May need toward higher end (1.4 g/kg) or even 1.6 g/kg
  • Very high training volumes create greater needs
  • Longer recovery periods needed

Masters/Older Athletes:

  • May benefit from higher intake (1.4-1.6 g/kg)
  • Reduced protein synthesis with aging
  • Need more to maintain muscle mass

Vegetarian/Vegan Endurance Athletes:

  • May need slightly higher total (1.4-1.6 g/kg)
  • Plant proteins have lower digestibility
  • Must combine complementary proteins
  • Consider supplementation (pea protein, soy protein)

Common Protein Sources for Endurance Athletes:

Animal Sources (per serving):

  • Chicken breast (3 oz): 26g protein
  • Greek yogurt (1 cup): 20g protein
  • Eggs (2 large): 12g protein
  • Salmon (3 oz): 22g protein
  • Milk (1 cup): 8g protein

Plant Sources (per serving):

  • Lentils (1 cup cooked): 18g protein
  • Chickpeas (1 cup): 15g protein
  • Quinoa (1 cup cooked): 8g protein
  • Peanut butter (2 Tbsp): 8g protein
  • Tofu (4 oz): 10g protein

Too Much Protein?

Upper Limits:

  • Generally safe: Up to 2.0 g/kg for athletes
  • Beyond benefit: >2.0 g/kg provides no additional advantage
  • Expensive calories: Protein costs more than carbs
  • May displace carbs: Endurance athletes need adequate carbohydrate

Potential Issues with Excessive Protein:

  • Kidney stress if pre-existing kidney disease
  • Dehydration if inadequate fluid intake
  • Inadequate carbohydrate for performance
  • Expensive and unnecessary

Protein Supplements:

When Useful:

  • Convenience (post-workout on the go)
  • Meeting needs when whole food difficult
  • Travel situations
  • Vegetarians/vegans supplementing plant proteins

Types:

  • Whey: Fast-digesting, post-workout ideal
  • Casein: Slow-digesting, before bed ideal
  • Soy: Plant-based complete protein
  • Pea protein: Plant-based, hypoallergenic

Not Necessary:

  • Whole foods can meet all needs
  • Supplements are convenience, not requirement
  • Focus on food first, supplement if needed

Exam Tips:

üéØ Critical facts for exam:

  • Endurance athletes: 1.2-1.4 g/kg/day ‚úì‚úì‚úì (MUST KNOW)
  • Sedentary RDA: 0.8 g/kg/day
  • Strength athletes: 1.6-2.0 g/kg/day (highest needs)
  • Recreational: 1.0-1.2 g/kg/day
  • Why endurance athletes need more: Muscle damage, protein oxidation during exercise, training adaptations
  • Timing matters: Distribute throughout day, post-exercise window important
  • Combine with carbs post-exercise: 3:1 or 4:1 ratio optimal
  • Quality matters: Complete proteins (all essential amino acids) best
  • Upper limit: >2.0 g/kg provides no additional benefit

Sample Exam Questions:

Q: What is the recommended protein intake for endurance athletes?

A: 1.2-1.4 g/kg body weight per day ‚úì

Q: Which population has the highest protein needs?

A: Strength/power athletes (1.6-2.0 g/kg) ‚úì

Q: Why do endurance athletes need more protein than the RDA?

A: Muscle damage from training, protein oxidation during prolonged exercise, adaptation needs ‚úì

Question 109: Joint Movement During Bicep Curl

Which joint movement occurs during a bicep curl?
‚úì CORRECT ANSWER:
Elbow flexion

Extended Explanation:

Understanding joint movements and the muscles that produce them is fundamental for exercise prescription, technique instruction, and exam success. The bicep curl is a classic exercise that every fitness professional must understand thoroughly.

Bicep Curl Biomechanics:

Primary Joint Action:

  • Elbow flexion ‚úì‚úì‚úì (bending the elbow)
  • Forearm moves toward upper arm
  • Angle at elbow joint decreases
  • Single-joint (isolation) exercise

Muscles Producing Elbow Flexion:

  • Biceps brachii: Primary mover (agonist)
  • Brachialis: Synergist (assists)
  • Brachioradialis: Synergist (especially with neutral grip)

Secondary Actions (Depending on Grip):

  • Supination: Rotating forearm to palm-up position
  • Biceps role: Also supinates forearm (secondary function)
  • Most evident: During hammer curl to supinated curl

Joint & Movement Terminology:

Anatomical Planes:

  • Bicep curl occurs in: Sagittal plane
  • Sagittal plane: Divides body into left and right
  • Movements: Flexion and extension

Phases of Bicep Curl:

  • Concentric phase (lifting):
    • Elbow flexion
    • Biceps shortening
    • Against gravity and resistance
  • Eccentric phase (lowering):
    • Elbow extension
    • Biceps lengthening (under tension)
    • Controlled lowering

Why NOT the Other Options?

Shoulder Flexion:

  • Movement: Arm moving forward/upward in front of body
  • Muscles: Anterior deltoid, pectoralis major (clavicular head)
  • NOT primary in bicep curl: Shoulder should remain relatively stable
  • If occurring: Indicates poor form (using momentum)

Wrist Extension:

  • Movement: Bending hand backward at wrist
  • NOT part of bicep curl: Wrist should stay neutral
  • If occurring: Poor form, can cause wrist strain

Shoulder Abduction:

  • Movement: Arm moving away from body to side
  • Muscles: Middle deltoid, supraspinatus
  • NOT in bicep curl: Different plane and movement
  • Different exercise: Lateral raise

Bicep Curl Variations & Joint Actions:

Standard Bicep Curl (Supinated):

  • Grip: Palms up (supinated)
  • Primary: Elbow flexion
  • Biceps: Maximally activated
  • Most common variation

Hammer Curl (Neutral Grip):

  • Grip: Palms facing each other (neutral)
  • Primary: Still elbow flexion
  • Emphasizes: Brachioradialis and brachialis more
  • Forearm development

Reverse Curl (Pronated):

  • Grip: Palms down (pronated)
  • Primary: Still elbow flexion
  • Emphasizes: Brachioradialis significantly
  • More challenging: Biceps at mechanical disadvantage

Preacher Curl:

  • Position: Arm supported on pad
  • Primary: Elbow flexion (same)
  • Isolates biceps: Prevents momentum/cheating
  • Shoulder stabilized

Common Bicep Curl Mistakes:

Technique Errors:

  • ‚ùå Swinging/using momentum: Shoulder flexion to help (cheating)
  • ‚ùå Elbows moving forward: Shoulder involvement reduces bicep work
  • ‚ùå Partial range of motion: Not fully extending or flexing
  • ‚ùå Too much weight: Forces poor form
  • ‚ùå Fast eccentric: Dropping weight instead of controlling
  • ‚ùå Hyperextending wrist: Wrist strain

Proper Bicep Curl Technique:

Setup:

  • Stand with feet shoulder-width apart
  • Core engaged, slight knee bend
  • Elbows close to sides
  • Neutral wrist position
  • Select appropriate weight

Execution:

  • Concentric (lifting):
    • Exhale
    • Flex elbows, curl weight up
    • Keep elbows stationary at sides
    • 2 second lifting phase
  • Peak contraction:
    • Brief squeeze at top
    • Full elbow flexion
  • Eccentric (lowering):
    • Inhale
    • Controlled lowering
    • 3 second lowering phase
    • Full extension at bottom

Programming Bicep Curls:

Exercise Classification:

  • Type: Single-joint (isolation)
  • Muscle group: Arms (elbow flexors)
  • Push/Pull: Pull movement
  • Order: Typically after compound exercises

Sets & Reps by Goal:

  • Hypertrophy (size): 3-4 sets √ó 8-12 reps
  • Muscular endurance: 2-3 sets √ó 15-20 reps
  • Strength: 3-5 sets √ó 6-8 reps (less common for biceps)

Frequency:

  • 2-3 times per week
  • 48 hours recovery between sessions
  • Can train more frequently with lower volume

Functional Relevance:

Daily Activities Involving Elbow Flexion:

  • Lifting groceries, children, objects
  • Bringing food/drink to mouth
  • Opening doors (pulling)
  • Using tools (hammering, sawing)
  • Sports (throwing, racquet sports)

Biceps Anatomy Review:

Biceps Brachii:

  • Two heads: Long head and short head
  • Origin: Scapula (shoulder blade)
  • Insertion: Radius (forearm)
  • Actions:
    • Primary: Elbow flexion ‚úì
    • Secondary: Forearm supination
    • Weak: Shoulder flexion

Synergist Muscles:

  • Brachialis:
    • Under biceps
    • Pure elbow flexor (no supination)
    • Active in all curl variations
  • Brachioradialis:
    • Forearm muscle
    • Especially active in neutral/pronated grips
    • Visible on radial side of forearm

Exam Tips:

üéØ Critical facts for exam:

  • Bicep curl primary joint action: Elbow flexion ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Primary muscle: Biceps brachii
  • Synergists: Brachialis, brachioradialis
  • Plane of motion: Sagittal
  • Exercise type: Single-joint (isolation)
  • Concentric phase: Elbow flexion (lifting)
  • Eccentric phase: Elbow extension (lowering)
  • NOT shoulder flexion (that's a cheat/momentum)
  • NOT wrist extension (wrist stays neutral)

Sample Exam Questions:

Q: What joint movement occurs during a bicep curl?

A: Elbow flexion ‚úì

Q: What is the primary muscle during a bicep curl?

A: Biceps brachii ‚úì

Q: In what plane does a bicep curl occur?

A: Sagittal plane ‚úì

Q: Is a bicep curl a single-joint or multi-joint exercise?

A: Single-joint (isolation exercise) ‚úì

Question 110: Post-Exercise Carbohydrate Intake for Glycogen Resynthesis

What is the recommended carbohydrate intake post-exercise for glycogen resynthesis?
‚úì CORRECT ANSWER:
1.0-1.2 g/kg body weight

Extended Explanation:

Proper post-exercise nutrition is critical for recovery, especially glycogen resynthesis. Understanding optimal carbohydrate timing and amounts is essential for athlete nutrition counseling.

Post-Exercise Carbohydrate Recommendations:

The 1.0-1.2 g/kg Standard:

  • Amount: 1.0-1.2 grams per kilogram body weight ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Timing: Within 30-60 minutes post-exercise (critical window)
  • Can repeat: Every 2 hours for 4-6 hours if needed
  • Example: 70 kg athlete needs 70-84 grams carbs immediately post-workout

Why Glycogen Resynthesis Matters:

Glycogen Depletion During Exercise:

  • Moderate exercise (60 min): 40-50% muscle glycogen depleted
  • Vigorous exercise (90 min): 70-90% depletion possible
  • Intense intervals: Rapid glycogen use
  • Must be replenished for next training session

Resynthesis Rates:

  • With optimal nutrition: 5-10% per hour
  • Without carbs: 1-2% per hour (very slow)
  • First 2 hours: FASTEST resynthesis (insulin-independent pathway active)
  • Complete replenishment: May take 24-48 hours depending on depletion

Optimal Timing - The "Glycogen Window":

30-60 Minute Post-Exercise Window:

  • Why critical: Muscle cells most insulin-sensitive
  • Enhanced uptake: Glucose transporters (GLUT4) mobilized to cell surface
  • Enzyme activity: Glycogen synthase highly active
  • Can double: Resynthesis rate compared to waiting 2+ hours

Continued Feeding Strategy:

  • Repeat 1.0-1.2 g/kg every 2 hours
  • Continue for 4-6 hours post-exercise
  • Total: 6-10 g/kg over 24 hours for complete replenishment
  • Example: 70 kg athlete needs 420-700g carbs total over 24 hours

Carbohydrate Type Matters:

High Glycemic Index (GI) Preferred Post-Exercise:

  • Rapid absorption: Quickly raises blood glucose
  • Strong insulin response: Drives glucose into muscle
  • Examples:
    • White bread, bagels
    • Sports drinks
    • Potatoes
    • Rice cakes
    • Pretzels
    • Dried fruit

Liquid vs. Solid:

  • Liquid carbs: Faster absorption, good immediately post-exercise
  • Examples: Sports drinks, chocolate milk, smoothies
  • Solid foods: Fine after initial liquid intake or if tolerated
  • Practical: Many athletes prefer liquids immediately (easier when appetite suppressed)

Adding Protein Enhances Glycogen Resynthesis:

Optimal Carb:Protein Ratio:

  • 3:1 or 4:1 ratio (carbs:protein) ‚úì
  • Example: 80g carbs + 20g protein
  • Why add protein:
    • Enhances insulin response
    • Supports muscle protein synthesis
    • May accelerate glycogen resynthesis
    • Comprehensive recovery (not just glycogen)

Practical Post-Exercise Meals/Snacks:

  • Chocolate milk: 1-2 cups (~45-90g carbs, 16-32g protein) - EXCELLENT option
  • Smoothie: Banana + berries + protein powder + milk
  • Bagel + peanut butter + banana
  • Greek yogurt + granola + fruit
  • Rice + chicken + vegetables
  • Recovery shake: Commercial products often 3:1 or 4:1 ratio

Special Situations:

Multiple Training Sessions Per Day:

  • Critical importance: <8 hours between sessions
  • Strategy: Immediate post-workout fueling mandatory
  • Amount: Use 1.2 g/kg (higher end) to maximize resynthesis
  • Repeat: Every 2 hours until next session

Single Daily Session with >24h Recovery:

  • Less urgent: More time for resynthesis
  • Still recommended: Post-workout nutrition within 2 hours
  • Can use: Regular meals meeting total daily carb needs
  • Total daily: 5-7 g/kg for moderate training, 7-10 g/kg for high-volume

Immediately Before Competition:

  • Last major meal 3-4 hours pre-competition
  • Small carb snack 30-60 min before (30-60g)
  • Ensure glycogen fully loaded from previous days

Individual Variability:

Factors Affecting Glycogen Resynthesis:

  • Training status: Trained athletes resynthesize faster
  • Muscle damage: Delayed resynthesis if significant damage
  • Insulin sensitivity: Better sensitivity = faster resynthesis
  • Age: Older athletes may have slower rates
  • Diet composition: Chronic low-carb diet may impair capacity

Common Mistakes:

  • ‚ùå Waiting too long to eat: Missing the optimal window
  • ‚ùå Not enough carbs: <0.8 g/kg inadequate
  • ‚ùå Too much fat post-workout: Slows carbohydrate absorption
  • ‚ùå Only protein shake: Need carbs for glycogen
  • ‚ùå Assuming one post-workout meal enough: May need multiple feedings

Exam Tips:

üéØ Critical facts for exam:

  • Post-exercise carb intake: 1.0-1.2 g/kg body weight ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Timing: Within 30-60 minutes (optimal window)
  • Can repeat every 2 hours
  • Combine with protein: 3:1 or 4:1 ratio (carb:protein)
  • High GI carbs preferred post-exercise
  • Why important: Maximizes glycogen resynthesis rate
  • Example: 70kg athlete needs 70-84g carbs immediately
  • Chocolate milk: Excellent post-exercise option

Sample Exam Questions:

Q: What is the recommended carbohydrate intake immediately post-exercise?

A: 1.0-1.2 g/kg body weight ‚úì

Q: What is the optimal timing for post-exercise carbohydrate intake?

A: Within 30-60 minutes after exercise ‚úì

Q: What is the recommended carbohydrate to protein ratio post-exercise?

A: 3:1 or 4:1 (carbohydrate:protein) ‚úì

Question 111: The Principle of Overload

The principle of overload states:
‚úì CORRECT ANSWER:
Body adapts when stressed beyond normal demands

Extended Explanation:

The overload principle is one of the fundamental training principles underlying all exercise adaptation. Understanding this concept is essential for program design and is frequently tested on ACSM exams.

The Overload Principle Defined:

  • Core concept: Body adapts when stressed BEYOND its current capacity ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Must progressively challenge the system to improve
  • Adaptation occurs to better handle future stress
  • Without overload: Only maintenance, no improvement
  • Too much overload: Overtraining, injury

How to Apply Overload (FITT Principle):

Increase Frequency:

  • Add more days per week
  • Example: 3 days ‚Üí 4 days per week

Increase Intensity:

  • Higher percentage of max (HR, 1RM, VO‚ÇÇmax)
  • Example: 70% max ‚Üí 80% max

Increase Time (Duration):

  • Longer exercise sessions
  • Example: 30 minutes ‚Üí 45 minutes

Change Type:

  • Different modalities or exercises
  • New stimulus creates adaptation

Practical Examples:

  • Cardio: Running faster, longer, or more often
  • Strength: Adding weight, reps, or sets
  • Flexibility: Stretching longer or deeper

Related Principles:

  • Progression: Gradually increasing overload
  • Specificity: Adaptations specific to stress applied
  • Reversibility: "Use it or lose it"
  • Individual differences: Response varies by person

Exam Tips:

üéØ Key points:

  • Overload = stress beyond current capacity ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Basis for ALL training adaptations
  • Apply via FITT variables
  • Must be progressive (gradual increases)
  • Without overload: No improvement

Question 112: Saturated Fat Intake Guidelines

What is the target range for saturated fat intake?
‚úì CORRECT ANSWER:
<10% of total calories

Extended Explanation:

Understanding dietary fat recommendations is essential for nutrition counseling and cardiovascular disease risk reduction. Saturated fat guidelines are heavily emphasized in ACSM materials.

Saturated Fat Guidelines:

  • AHA/USDA recommendation: <10% of total calories ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • More aggressive goal: <7% for CVD risk reduction
  • Example calculation (2000 kcal diet):
    • 10% = 200 kcal from saturated fat
    • 200 kcal √∑ 9 kcal/g fat = 22 grams saturated fat

Why Limit Saturated Fat?

  • Raises LDL cholesterol: "Bad" cholesterol
  • Increases CVD risk: Heart disease, stroke
  • Inflammatory effects: Promotes systemic inflammation
  • Dose-response: More saturated fat = higher LDL

Common Sources of Saturated Fat:

  • Animal products: Red meat, full-fat dairy, butter, cheese
  • Tropical oils: Coconut oil, palm oil
  • Processed foods: Baked goods, fried foods
  • Hidden sources: Coffee creamers, sauces

What to Replace Saturated Fat With:

  • Unsaturated fats: Olive oil, avocado, nuts
  • Fatty fish: Salmon, mackerel (omega-3s)
  • Lean proteins: Chicken, turkey, fish
  • Plant-based: Legumes, tofu

Total Fat Recommendations:

  • Total fat: 20-35% of total calories
  • Saturated: <10% (within total fat)
  • Trans fat: As low as possible (ideally 0)
  • Unsaturated: Remainder (majority of fat intake)

Practical Tips:

  • Choose lean cuts of meat
  • Select low-fat or fat-free dairy
  • Cook with olive or canola oil instead of butter
  • Read labels: aim for <5g saturated fat per serving
  • Limit red meat to 1-2 times per week

Exam Tips:

üéØ Critical facts:

  • Saturated fat: <10% of total calories ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • More aggressive: <7% for CVD risk
  • Raises LDL cholesterol (bad cholesterol)
  • Replace with unsaturated fats
  • Sources: red meat, full-fat dairy, tropical oils

Question 113: Muscular Power Definition

Which of the following best describes muscular power?
‚úì CORRECT ANSWER:
Force √ó velocity (work/time)

Extended Explanation:

Understanding the distinction between strength, power, and endurance is fundamental for exercise prescription and program design.

Muscular Power Defined:

  • Power = Force √ó Velocity ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Or: Power = Work / Time
  • Definition: How fast you can produce force
  • Explosive strength: Rapid force production
  • Speed-strength: Combination of speed and strength

Power vs. Strength vs. Endurance:

Strength:

  • Definition: Maximum force regardless of time
  • Example: 1-rep max squat
  • Measured: In pounds, kilograms, or newtons

Power:

  • Definition: Force √ó Velocity (how fast you produce force)
  • Example: Vertical jump height, Olympic lifts
  • Measured: In watts

Endurance:

  • Definition: Ability to sustain contractions
  • Example: Maximum reps at submaximal weight
  • Measured: In repetitions or time

Examples of Power Movements:

  • Vertical jump: Rapid lower body power
  • Medicine ball throw: Upper body power
  • Olympic lifts: Snatch, clean & jerk (whole body power)
  • Sprinting: Rapid leg power production
  • Plyometrics: Box jumps, depth jumps
  • Kettlebell swings: Hip power

Training for Power:

Power Training Variables:

  • Load: 30-60% of 1RM (lighter than strength training)
  • Reps: 3-5 per set (low reps)
  • Sets: 3-5 sets
  • Rest: 3-5 minutes (full recovery for max power output)
  • Tempo: EXPLOSIVE concentric, controlled eccentric
  • Frequency: 2-3 days per week

Why Power Declines Fastest with Age:

  • Type II (fast-twitch) fibers: Atrophy preferentially with aging
  • Rate: ~3-4% per year after age 30
  • Compare to strength: ~1-2% per year (slower decline)
  • Functional impact: Affects quick movements, balance recovery
  • Importance: Power training especially important for older adults

Practical Applications:

  • Athletes: Power essential for most sports
  • Older adults: Power for fall prevention, functional tasks
  • General population: Improves athletic performance, daily function

Exam Tips:

üéØ Critical facts:

  • Power = Force √ó Velocity (or Work/Time) ‚úì‚úì‚úì
  • Explosive strength, rate of force production
  • Declines fastest with aging (3-4% per year)
  • Train with 30-60% 1RM, explosive tempo, 3-5 reps
  • Examples: vertical jump, Olympic lifts, sprints
  • Different from strength (max force regardless of time)
